    203

    ○张御史神政记

    　　始予读先汉等史，见诸循吏事，有政通神明，精感天地者，未尝不太息钦想，以为后世所未有也，乃今于慈溪张公而复见之。公名昺，字仲明，都御史楷之子也。成化中，始以进士知铅山县。初，县有卖薪者嗜食鳝，得薪直，以其半市鳝，命妻烹而食之。一日，自市归，乘饥恣啖，少时腹痛而死。邻保疑妻毒杀之，执送官，拷讯无他状，狱不能具，械系踰年。公初莅任，尝白昼登堂，忽覩门外有绿袍乌巾者冉冉而入，左右悉不觉也。及行案前长揖，公迎之坐，乃曰：「公毋惧，吾非人，实邑中某乡之土神也。乡有寃狱未白，知公精明果断，与神明通，必能雪之。」公问其事，神曰：「吾乡民某甲之妻，以杀夫系狱，此人本中鳝毒而殂，非妇罪也。公欲验之，但置鳝水瓮中，有昂头出水二三寸者必杀之，试烹以啖他囚而死，则其事白矣。」言讫不见。公异之。诘旦，召阖境渔者命捕鳝，得数百斤。

    Explanation

    203

    ○The Tale of Zhang the Magistrate's Divine Governance

    At first, I read the histories of the Former Han dynasty and others, encountering the stories of various upright officials. Some of these officials were so effective in their governance that they seemed to communicate with the divine, and their spiritual power could move heaven and earth. I never fail to sigh and admire deeply, thinking it was something not seen in later generations. But now, in Zhang Gong of Cixi, I see it again. His name is Bing, and his courtesy name is Zhongming. He is the son of Duyushi Kai. During the Chenghua period, he first served as the magistrate of Qianshan County through the imperial examination.

    Initially, there was a woodcutter in the county who loved to eat eels. After selling firewood, he would use half the money to buy eels, and he ordered his wife to cook and eat them. One day, after returning from the market, he ate them greedily when he was hungry. Shortly after, he died of abdominal pain. The neighbors suspected that his wife had poisoned him, and they arrested her and took her to the authorities. No other evidence was found, and the case could not be resolved, so she was imprisoned for over a year.

    When Zhang Gong first took office, he once climbed the hall during the day. Suddenly, he saw someone dressed in green robes and a black hat slowly entering from outside the door. Those around him did not notice at all. When this person came to the front of the desk, he bowed deeply, and Zhang Gong greeted him and invited him to sit. The visitor said, "Do not be afraid, I am not a human, but the local god of a certain village in this county. There is an unjust case that has not been cleared. I know you are wise and decisive, and you communicate with the divine. You will surely clear it up."

    Zhang Gong asked about the matter, and the god said, "The wife of a man named Jiǎ in my village is in prison for killing her husband, but he actually died from eating eels, not because of her." Zhang Gong wanted to verify this, so he placed the eels in a water jar. Any eel that raised its head above the water by two or three inches should be killed. If cooking and feeding them to other prisoners caused them to die, then the case would be cleared. After speaking, the god disappeared. Zhang Gong found this strange. The next day, he summoned all the fishermen in the county to catch eels, and they caught several hundred pounds.

    204

    如神言试之，得昂头者凡七，设釜于堂，召此妇面烹之，出死囚于庭与食，纔下咽 【 纔下咽　原本「下」作「不」，据说库本改。】 ，便称腹痛，俄仆地死。公谓妇曰：「汝寃白矣。」遂释之。徐访其乡，果有神祠，视其像，正所见也。又甲嫁女于乙 【 又甲嫁女于乙　原本「嫁」作「妇」，据说库本改。】 ，抵乙门，揭幕视之，则空舆而已。乙谓甲欺己，诉于县，甲又以戕其女，互相争执。前令逮媒从诸人鞫之，皆云：「女实升舆，不知何以失去。」令不能决。

    Explanation

    204

    As the god commanded, they tested it, and those who raised their heads were seven in total. They set a pot in the hall and summoned this woman to be cooked in front of everyone. They brought out a condemned prisoner in the courtyard and gave them food; as soon as they swallowed, they immediately claimed to have abdominal pain and soon fell to the ground and died. The magistrate said to the woman, "Your innocence is now clear." He then released her. Slowly investigating her hometown, they indeed found a shrine, and upon looking at the image, it was exactly what was seen. Also, A married her daughter to B; when they arrived at B's door and lifted the curtain to see, it was just an empty carriage. B accused A of deceiving him and sued in the county, and A also accused B of killing her daughter, leading to a prolonged and contentious dispute. The previous magistrate arrested the matchmaker and others and interrogated them; all said that the daughter indeed boarded the carriage, but they did not know how she was lost. The magistrate could not resolve the case.

    205

    公至，偶以勘田均税出郊， 【 初太祖平陈氏，过铅山，定其税额，偶积余税，加于数百亩田上，有亩至数石者，以是公往看视，为奏均之。】 行至邑界，有树大数十抱，荫占二十余亩，其下不堪禾麦。公欲伐之以广田，从者咸谏，以为此树乃神所栖，百姓稍失瞻敬，便至死病，明府不可易视也。公不听，移文邻邑，约共伐之，其令惧祸不从，父老吏卒复交口谏沮，而公执愈坚。期日率数十夫，戎服鼓吹而往，未至数百步，有衣冠者三人拜谒道左曰：「我等树神也，栖息于此有年矣，幸公垂仁相舍。」公叱之，忽不见。

    Explanation

    205

    When the official arrived, he happened to go out into the suburbs to inspect the land and equalize the tax. [At the beginning, when the First Emperor pacified Chen Shi, he passed through Yanshan and set the tax rate. There was an excess of tax that was added to several hundred acres of land, with some acres taxed at several shi. The official went to inspect this, and upon seeing it, he reported to the emperor to have the tax rates adjusted.] As he approached the county boundary, he saw a tree with a trunk several dozen chi in circumference, casting shade over more than twenty acres. The area beneath the tree was unsuitable for growing wheat or millet. The official wanted to cut it down to expand the farmland. All his attendants advised against it, saying that this tree was the abode of a deity. If the people lost their reverence, they would die of illness. The magistrate cannot treat it lightly. The official did not listen and sent a document to the neighboring county, requesting their assistance in cutting down the tree. The magistrate of the neighboring county feared disaster and refused to comply. The elders, officials, and soldiers again strongly advised against it, but the official's determination grew stronger. On the appointed day, he led dozens of men, dressed in military attire and accompanied by music, to the site. As they approached within a few hundred steps, three men dressed in formal attire appeared on the side of the road and bowed to greet him, saying, "We are the tree spirits, and we have resided here for many years. We humbly request your mercy and ask you to spare us." The official rebuked them, and they suddenly disappeared.

    206

    命夫运斤，树有血出，众惧欲止，公乃手自斧之以为倡，凡三百方断其树。树颠有巨巢，巢中有三妇人堕地，冥然欲绝。命左右掖而灌之以汤，良久始苏，问何以在是，妇曰：「昔年为狂风吹至此，身在高楼与三少年欢宴，所食皆美馔，时时俯瞰楼下，城市历历在目，而无阶可下。少年往来率自空中飞腾，不知乃居树巢也。」公悉访其家人还之，中一人正甲所失女，自言在舆中为妖摄去，其讼遂解。公以其木修公廨数处，而所荫地复为良田。

    Explanation

    206

    The official commanded the man to chop the tree, and as he did so, the tree bled as if it were wounded. The crowd was afraid and wanted to stop, but the official personally took up the axe and began chopping to set an example. It took three hundred chops before the tree finally fell. At the top of the tree was a large nest, and inside the nest were three women who fell to the ground, unconscious and nearly dead. The official ordered his attendants to support them and pour hot water over them. After a long time, they began to revive and asked how they had come to be there. The women explained, "Last year, we were blown here by a strong wind. We were in a high building, enjoying a feast with three young men. Everything we ate was delicious food. We often looked down at the city below, which was clearly visible, but there was no way to go down. The young men came and went, flying through the air, and we did not know we were living in a tree nest." The official thoroughly searched for their families and returned them. One of them was the girl that person A had lost, who said she was taken away by a demon while in a carriage. The lawsuit was thus resolved. The official used the wood to repair several public buildings, and the land under the shade of the tree became good farmland again.

    207

    由是悉毁诸淫祠在境内者无遗，独乡落一祠，民秘之获存。他日，公以事经其地，梦神恳曰：「公姑恕我。」翌日召乡民，责令毁之。神忽降于邻邑小民曰：「吾被张公毁庙，无可寓寄。公正人，吾不敢犯，愿借片地暂居。公去，祠可复也。汝不吾从，五日内必及祸。」民初不信，未三日，果烦懑吐逆，神乃降，家人罗拜，为之立庙 【 为之立庙　原本「庙」作「厢」，据说库本改。】 。有道士善隐形术，多淫人妇女。公擒至，痛鞭之，了无所苦，已而并其形不见。

    Explanation

    207

    Zhang Gong thus completely destroyed all unauthorized temples within his domain, leaving none behind. Only one temple in the rural areas remained, which the villagers secretly preserved. One day, as Zhang Gong passed through the area on business, he dreamed of the god earnestly saying, "Please forgive me, I was destroyed by Zhang Gong." The next day, he summoned the villagers and ordered them to destroy the temple. The god suddenly descended upon the common people in the neighboring town, saying, "I was destroyed by Zhang Gong, and now I have nowhere to stay. You are an upright person, and I dare not offend you. I wish to borrow a small piece of land to stay temporarily. When you leave, the temple can be restored. If you do not follow me, disaster will surely come within five days." The villagers initially did not believe him, but within three days, the man who had been summoned became anxious and distressed, vomiting. The god then descended, and the family prostrated themselves, and they built a temple for him. There was a Taoist priest skilled in the art of invisibility, who often seduced women. Zhang Gong captured him, severely whipped him, and he suffered no pain. Afterward, his form disappeared.

    208

    公托以他出，径驰诣其居縳归，用印于背，然后鞭之，乃随声呼嘷，竟死杖下。邑寡妇唯一子，采薪于山，为虎所啖。邻居恶少欲以难事窘公，代妇书牒，使投之县，称欲得虎抵罪，公视牒笑曰：「奸民欲窘我乎？」与妇期五日来。乃斋戒作文祭城隍，大畧言：「神为一邑主，不能御灾捍患而纵虎食人，今与神约，五日内必驱虎伏辜 【 五日内必驱虎伏辜　原本「驱虎伏辜」作「驱伏虎辜」，据说库本改。】 ，否则撤其庙而更置之。」后五日，天未明，梦神告曰：「虎至矣。

    Explanation

    208

    The official pretended to go out on some pretext, then rushed to the suspect's residence, bound them, and brought them back. He stamped a seal on their back, then beat them. The suspect screamed in agony until they died under the whip. A widow from the town had only one son, who was gathering firewood in the mountains when he was eaten by a tiger. A mischievous neighbor wanted to trap the official, so he wrote a petition on behalf of the widow and submitted it to the county office, claiming that he wanted to see the tiger punished. The official looked at the petition and laughed, saying, "Are these wicked people trying to trap me?" He agreed to meet with the widow in five days.

    He then fasted and wrote an offering to the city deity, saying, "You are the master of this town, yet you cannot prevent disasters and protect the people from harm, allowing the tiger to eat them. Today, I make a pact with you: within five days, I will drive the tiger to confess and take responsibility. Otherwise, I will demolish your temple and replace it with another." Five days later, before dawn, he dreamed that the deity told him, "The tiger is coming."

    209

    」公惊起，佩弓矢升堂，命启门，有二虎入伏庭下，若有人守之者。公厉声叱曰：「吾良民之子而汝食之，法当抵死。二虎有非伤人者退。」一虎起，绕伏虎一匝，低尾而出，其一不动。公素善射，拔所佩箭，三发而三中其首，因命隶卒乱鞭杀之。召妇人，归以虎尸。自是所在喧传，目为神人。三年以政最擢监察御史。今致仕家居，杜门谢事，足不入公府，隐然为乡邦重望，君子惜其位不满德云。予闻得公事于其乡人，因此为记，后之传循吏者或将有考焉。右张御史神政记，予弟子远作，录之以终吾编。

    　　庚巳编十卷 （明）陆粲撰；谭棣华，陈稼禾点校 元明史料笔记丛刊 北京市:中华书局，1987[民76]1997湖北第2刷

    Explanation

    209

    The official started up in alarm, wearing his bow and arrows, he ascended to the hall, ordered the door to be opened, and there entered two tigers that lay hidden in the courtyard, as if guarded by someone. The official sternly rebuked, saying: "My good citizens' children, and you have eaten them, by law, you should be executed. If you did not harm humans, retreat." One tiger rose, circled the other tiger once, lowered its tail and left, while the other remained motionless. The official was skilled at archery, drew his arrows, fired three times and hit the head each time, then ordered his subordinates to kill them with whips. He called the woman, and she returned with the tiger corpses. From then on, the story spread widely, and he was regarded as a divine person. After three years, for his excellent governance, he was promoted to the position of Inspector of the Censorate. Now retired and living at home, he closed his door and declined official business, never entered the government office, becoming a respected figure in the local area, gentlemen lamented that his position did not match his virtue. I heard from the people of his hometown and wrote this record, future accounts of upright officials may find it useful. Right here is the Record of the Divine Governance of Inspector Zhang, written by my disciple Yuan, recorded to conclude my compilation.

    Appendix:

    Zuo Zhuan Annotations and Notes in Five Volumes (Submitted by the Governor of Zhejiang)

    By Lu Can of the Ming dynasty, who was also known as Ziyu and hailed from Changzhou. He passed the imperial examinations in 1546 (Jiajing bingxu) and held the position of censor in the Ministry of Works. Later, he was exiled to a postal station for impeaching Zhang Cong and Gui E. He served as the magistrate of Yongxin County until his death. His biography is recorded in the Ming Shi (official history of the Ming dynasty). This compilation consists of five volumes: the first three volumes correct Du Yu's annotations, the second volume corrects Kong Yingda's exegesis, and the fifth volume corrects Lu Deming's phonetic interpretations of the Zuo Zhuan. It draws on the theories of various scholars and occasionally includes the author's own opinions, providing significant benefit to scholars of classical Chinese. Gu Yanwu, in his Rizhilu, wrote in the section on correcting the Zuo Zhuan annotations: "Any points already addressed by Mr. Shao, Mr. Fu, and Mr. Lu are not included here. 'Shao' refers to Shao Bao, 'Left Zhuan Zhong' to Fu Xun, and 'Lu' is Can himself. This shows that Yanwu highly values this work." Can also wrote Chunqiu Zuo Zhuan Jue in two volumes, arguing that the Zuo Zhuan was written by a Warring States period author, and that Liu Xin further tampered with it, resulting in a work that is often shallow and does not conform to the proper path, sometimes containing strange and peculiar statements that run after the fringe. Can attributed the Zuo Zhuan to the Warring States period, likely because Cheng Hao and Cheng Yi stated that "Lao" (腊) was a Qin ritual and "shuzhang" (庶长) was a Qin official, which he considered rigid and unyielding. He attributed the tampering to Liu Xin, likely because Lin Li argued that any mention of "junzi" (君子) in the Zuo Zhuan was a word of Liu Xin, with no evidence to support this claim. This led Can to indulge in lofty speculation, still following the speculative habits of Ming scholars, which is like adding a foot to a snake. Therefore, only this compilation is included, while Chunqiu Zuo Zhuan Jue is mentioned separately.

    Chunqiu Hu Shizhuan Bianyi in Two Volumes (Submitted by the Governor of Jiangsu)

    211

    明陆粲撰前有自序谓胡氏说经或失于过求词不厌烦而圣人之意愈晦故着此以辨论之大旨主于信经而不信例其言曰不以正大之情观春秋而曲生意义将焉所不至矣又曰昔之君子有言春秋无达例如以例言则有时而穷惟其有时而穷故求其说而不得从而为之辞又曰春秋褒善贬恶不易之法今用此法以诛人又忽用此说以赏人使后世求之而莫识其意是直舞文吏所为而谓圣人为之乎其抉摘说经之弊皆洞中症结其例皆先列胡传于前而以已说纠正于后如以春秋始于隐公独取欧阳氏之说以为远而难明者不修而不取胡氏罪平王之说于纪履緰来逆女以为为齐侯灭纪葬伯姬书而不取胡氏逆女必亲使大夫非正之说于遂以夫人妇姜至自齐以为声姜敬嬴穆姜皆称妇以文宣成皆有母称妇以别于君母而不取胡氏贬称妇以见恶之说于齐人来归郓讙龟阴田以为鲁及齐平而归田不必以夹谷之会悉归功于孔子三传家语及史说皆未足据而不取胡氏所称摄相郄齐兵之说如此者凡六十余条大抵明白正大足以破繁文曲说之弊自元延佑二年立胡传于学宫明永乐纂修大全沿而不改世儒遂相沿墨守莫敢异同惟粲及袁仁始显攻其失其后若俞汝言焦袁熹张自超等踵加论辨乃推阐无余虽卷帙不多其有功于春秋固不尠也朱彝尊经义考作四卷注云未见此本祗上下二卷实无所阙佚殆彝尊考之未审欤（四库全书总目?经部?春秋类）

    Explanation

    211

    Ming dynasty, by Lu Can, compiled. With a preface written by the author, he says that Hu's interpretation of the classics sometimes errs in being overly detailed and verbose, making the meaning of the sages more obscure. Therefore, he writes this to debate and clarify it. The main point is to believe in the text of the classics rather than in established rules. He says, "Not viewing the Spring and Autumn Annals with a straightforward and honest perspective but instead creating twisted meanings, where will it not lead?" He also says, "Ancient scholars said that the Spring and Autumn Annals have no fixed rules. Because it is sometimes difficult to understand, one might seek an explanation and then fabricate a reason." He further says, "The Spring and Autumn Annals have an unchanging method of praising good deeds and criticizing bad ones. Now using this method to condemn someone, yet suddenly using this same method to praise someone else, making it impossible for future generations to understand its meaning. Is this not what a petty bureaucrat would do, and to call it the work of a sage?" His critique of the flaws in interpreting the classics hits the core issues. His examples are all presented by first listing Hu's interpretation and then correcting it with his own. For example, he takes the view that the Spring and Autumn Annals begin with Duke Yin, adopting only the explanation of Ouyang Xiu, considering it distant and difficult to understand, and not revising it. He does not accept Hu's criticism of Duke Ping of Zhou, arguing that the Spring and Autumn Annals record the death of Lady Boji because Duke Qi of Lu had destroyed the state of Ji. He also does not accept Hu's claim that the Spring and Autumn Annals require the king to personally send a minister to marry a daughter, considering it incorrect. He argues that the Spring and Autumn Annals refer to the arrival of Lady Jiang, Lady Jing, and Lady Mu as "Lady" to distinguish them from the king's mother, and he does not accept Hu's view that the Spring and Autumn Annals use the term "Lady" to show disapproval. He also argues that the Spring and Autumn Annals record the return of land to Lu from Qi without attributing all the credit to Confucius at the Jiaxu conference. He cites the Three Transmissions, Family Language, and historical accounts, finding them insufficient to support Hu's claims. He lists over sixty examples of Hu's interpretations, all of which are clear and straightforward, sufficient to break the tradition of verbose and twisted explanations. Since the Yuan dynasty's Yanyou period, Hu's commentary was established in the imperial academy, and during the Ming dynasty's Yongle period, the Great Compendium was compiled, following this tradition without change. Scholars thus followed tradition blindly and dared not differ. Only Can and Yuan Ren began to openly criticize its flaws. Later, people like Yu Ruyan, Jiao Yuanxi, Zhang Zichao, and others followed in their footsteps and added their own criticisms, fully expounding on the issues. Although the volume is not large, it has made significant contributions to the Spring and Autumn Annals. Zhu Yizun's Jingyi Kaoshi lists it as four volumes, noting that he has not seen this edition, only the upper and lower two volumes, which are complete. Perhaps Zhu Yizun's examination was not thorough.

    212

    左氏春秋镌二卷（浙江巡抚采进本）

    明陆粲撰粲有左传附注已著录是编乃其由工科给事中坐劾张璁桂萼谪都匀驿丞时途中所作皆纠正左氏议论之失亦柳宗元非国语之类然于左氏释经之谬辟之可也至记事记言但各从其实事乖言谬咎在古人与纪载者无与也亦谓之镌左则非其罪矣甚哉其固也（四库全书总目?经部?春秋类存目）

    Zuo Zhuan Jue (镌二卷) (Two Volumes of Critique of the Zuo Zhuan) (Submitted by the Zhejiang Provincial Governor)

    Written by Lu Can of the Ming dynasty. Lu Can previously authored Zuo Zhuan Fu Zhu (《左傳附注》), which has already been recorded. This edition was written by him when he was exiled to the position of postal station officer in Duyun after being impeached as a censor in the Ministry of Works for criticizing Zhang Cong and Gui E. It corrects the errors in the arguments of the Zuo Zhuan, similar to Liu Zongyuan's critique of the Guoyu. However, it is possible to correct the errors in the Zuo Zhuan's interpretation of the Spring and Autumn Annals. Recording events and words should follow the actual facts; if the facts are wrong or the words are erroneous, the blame lies with the ancient people and the recorders, not with the present writer. Calling it a critique of the Zuo Zhuan would not be a fault. How stubborn it is! (Catalog of the Siku Quanshu? Jingbu (经部)? Chunqiu (春秋) class? Entry)

    213

    陆子余集八卷（两淮盐政采进本）

    明陆粲撰粲有左传附注巳著录是集凡文七卷诗一卷粲早入词馆负盛名洎官工科以劾张璁桂萼偃蹇终身然亦缘是息意邱园研心经史学问具有根柢又为王鏊门人明史粲本传称其少谒鏊鏊异之曰此子必以文名天下其授受亦有端绪徐时行序称其出入左氏司马迁无论魏晋彭年序以为专法马班雄深雅健东汉诸家所不及推奬颇为太过至黄宗羲明文海云贞山文秀美平顺不起波澜得之王文恪居多乃欧阳氏之支流则平心之论当之无媿色矣其忆父诗一首明诗综云七岁所作然风格老成不应至是疑或有所夸饰至于担夫谣之类有香山新乐府遗音赠别王直夫二首之类亦绰有风格尤未可以篇什无多遂谓曾子固不能诗也（四库全书总目?集部?别集类）

    Explanation

    213

    Lu Zi Yu Ji (陆子余集), Eight Volumes (Collected by the Salt Administration of the Two Huai Regions)

    Compiled by Lu Can of the Ming Dynasty. Lu Can has already been recorded for his annotations on the Zuo Zhuan (Left Commentary). This collection includes seven volumes of prose and one volume of poetry. Lu Can was renowned for his early entry into the Hanlin Academy, where he gained a great reputation. Later, he was appointed to the Ministry of Works but was dismissed due to his impeachment of Zhang Cong and Gui E, leading to a life of stubbornness and disfavor. However, this also led him to withdraw from official life and retire to the countryside, where he devoted himself to studying the classics and history, laying a solid foundation for his scholarship. He was also a disciple of Wang Ao, a famous Ming dynasty statesman and scholar. The biography of Lu Can in the Ming History records that when he first visited Wang Ao, Wang found him extraordinary and said, "This boy will surely become famous for his writing in the world." His learning and teaching had a clear lineage. Xu Shixing, in his preface, notes that Lu Can drew inspiration from the Zuo Zhuan and the works of Sima Qian, regardless of the Wei and Jin periods. Peng Nian, in his preface, believed that Lu Can specialized in the styles of Sima Qian and Ban Gu, producing powerful, profound, elegant, and robust writing that surpassed all the writers of the Eastern Han period. He was highly praised, though perhaps somewhat excessively. Huang Zongxi, in his Ming Wenhai (Ocean of Ming Literature), commented that Lu Can's writing was beautiful and smooth, with little fluctuation, much of which came from the influence of Wang Ao. It is also considered a branch of the tradition of Ouyang Xiu. This is an impartial and fair evaluation, and Lu Can can face it without any shame. One of his poems, "Memories of My Father," is said to have been written when he was seven years old, though the style is mature and sophisticated, suggesting that it may have been exaggerated. As for his other works, such as "The Ballad of the Porters" and the two farewell poems to Wang Zhifu, they also show a distinct style, and it would be unfair to dismiss his poetry simply because he did not write many poems, as some have suggested that Ceng Ziqi, a famous Song dynasty writer, was not particularly skilled at poetry.

    陆粲左传附注五卷【粲读左氏注疏若释文于训诂音切可疑者辄以已见及他书之说可证据者附注其下】又左氏镌二卷【以左氏非左丘明故为言毕浅不中于道疑战国时私淑于孔子者所为因为之镌以晓示学者】又胡传辨疑二卷【嘉靖辛卯序】（四库全书?史部?目录类?经籍之属?千顷堂书目卷二）

    Translation

    Lu Can's Zuo Zhuan Fu Zhu in five volumes and Chun Qiu Zuo Shi Gui in two volumes (from the Four Treasuries? History Department? Official Histories? Ming Shi? Volume 96)
    Lu Can's Geng Si Bian in ten volumes (from the Four Treasuries? History Department? Official Histories? Ming Shi? Volume 98)
    Lu Can's Zhen Shan Ji in twelve volumes (from the Four Treasuries? History Department? Official Histories? Ming Shi? Volume 99)

    Lu Can, courtesy name Zi Yu, was from Changzhou. He passed the imperial examination in 1536 (Jiajing bing xu year) and was selected as a Shu Ji Shi, later changing to a position in the Works Department as a Censor. He was bold and outspoken. At the time, Zhang Cong and Gui E were wielding power. Lu Can impeached their crimes, and the emperor immediately dismissed them. They were later reinstated, and Huo Tao's words led to their recall. Lu Can was imprisoned and demoted to the position of postal station manager in Duzhen, then promoted to the position of magistrate in Yongxin. He captured hundreds of bandits, driving away the wicked and cunning. He requested to return home due to his mother's illness, and after her death, he suffered greatly from grief, dying before completing the mourning period. Lu Can loved learning and was broadly knowledgeable about ancient and modern times. He wrote works that were published and circulated.

    Lu Can, courtesy name Jun Ming, was from Changzhou. He passed the imperial examination and was promoted to the position of Censor. He was dismissed for his remonstrance and demoted in 1536 (Jiajing twelfth year). He was later reassigned to the position of magistrate in Yongxin. He governed with strict and clear policies, making officials upright and the people fearful. There were several dozen gangs of evildoers who harmed the good. He investigated and punished them according to the law. At the time, bandits were rampant in the region, and discontented people were rising in groups. Lu Can captured and executed them all. He particularly valued education, respected the elderly, and promoted village regulations. The people still speak of him today [according to An Zhi] (from the Four Treasuries? History Department? Regional Histories? Prefectures and Counties? Jiangxi Tong Zhi? Volume 61).

    陆粲【字浚明长洲人嘉靖丙戌进士选庶吉士补工科给事中直言忤旨谪驿丞令永新有异政以养母告归浚明学术至深其所觧义儒者多不逮屡抗疏言事诋斥用事者则尤烈烈引义虽流窜戮辱气未尝折下也可不谓骨鲠臣哉】（四库全书?子部?类书类?万姓统谱卷一百十一）

    陆粲
    粲字子余一字浚明长洲人嘉靖丙戍进士选庻吉士改授工科给事中坐劾张桂谪都匀驿丞迁永新知县有贞山稾【顾元言云给事与屠文升袁永之王汝化同时入馆并有盛名亦李唐四杰之流　诗话嘉靖间元老类皆延揽宾客虽贵溪分宜亦然惟张桂专与文士为雠丙戍庻常一笔画扫贞山又多论劾困抑终身然晚极田庐文酒之乐菽园著述传诵艺林比之二柄臣孰得孰失耶】（四库全书?集部?总集类?明诗综卷四十五）

    Explanation

    216

    Lu Can, also known as Junming, was a native of Changzhou. He passed the imperial examination in 1556 during the Jiajing era and was selected as a scholar-official candidate. He was appointed to the position of a censor in the Ministry of Works but spoke frankly against the emperor's wishes, which led to his demotion to the position of a postmaster in Yongxin, where he had distinguished governance. Later, he resigned to take care of his mother. Lu Can was deeply learned, and his interpretations of classical texts surpassed those of many Confucian scholars. He frequently submitted memorials to speak on matters of state and criticized those in power, particularly harshly. Despite being exiled, killed, or humiliated, he never lost his spirit. Can he not be called a loyal and outspoken minister? (Four Treasuries, Subsidiary Section, Anthology of Names, Volume 111)

    Lu Can, also known as Ziyu and Junming, was a native of Changzhou. He passed the imperial examination in 1556 during the Jiajing era and was selected as a scholar-official candidate. He was then appointed to the position of a censor in the Ministry of Works. He was demoted to the position of a postmaster in Duyun after impeaching Zhang Gui and later transferred to the position of magistrate in Yongxin. He had a collection of writings called Zhen Shan Gao (Essays of Zhen Shan). According to Gu Yuan Yan, he and Tu Wensheng, Yuan Yongzhi, and Wang Ruhua were contemporaries and had great reputations, similar to the Four Great Writers of the Tang dynasty. During the Jiajing era, influential figures generally sought out guests, even those in Guxi and Fenxi, but Zhang Gui was particularly hostile towards literati. In 1556, he swept away Zhen Shan with a single stroke and frequently criticized and suppressed scholars, leading to their lifelong suppression. However, in his later years, he enjoyed the pleasures of rural life and literary gatherings, and his works were widely circulated and praised in the literary circles. Comparing him to the two powerful ministers, who gained and who lost? (Four Treasuries, Collection Section, Anthology of Ming Poetry, Volume 45)

    前工科给事中赠太常寺少卿贞山陆公墓碑　　（明）王世贞 撰

    （四库全书?集部?别集类?弇州四部稿续稿卷一百三十四）

    陆公者讳粲字子余一字浚明其先汉豫章都尉烈尝为吴令而其人思之及死葬吴之胥屏亭遂留家焉至宋季而有十九朝议者避兵徙长洲之陈湖遂复为长洲人公故家陈湖而时时读书贞山中学者尊称之曰贞山先生当陆之盛几倾邑而族父少保完者故立大功领太宰大司马重于天下公守诸生絶不附依之及少保败而独公皭然乃自以经术秇文为少傅王文恪公所知赏曰是子也成将掩我因出其所撰着属公核之间有所异同輙志于后曰陆秀才谓我当云云文恪公之卒而公尚未离诸生也公亦不以此自名而其举于应天再举会试皆以春秋冠其经生比对策故辅石文介公珤

    Explanation

    217

    Tombstone Inscription for Former Censor of the Ministry of Works, Posthumously Awarded the Rank of Deputy Director of the Imperial Sacrifices Office, Mr. Lu of Zhen Mountain (Wang Shizhen of the Ming dynasty)

    (Written in the Siku Quanshu, Collection of Miscellaneous Works, Volume 134 of the Yanzhou Four Departments Collection Continuation)

    Mr. Lu, whose name was Càn, courtesy name Zǐyú, and another courtesy name Jùnmíng, had ancestors who were Commandant of Yuzhang during the Han dynasty, named Liè, who once served as Governor of Wu. After his death, the people of Wu remembered him and buried him in Xūpíngting, thus he remained in Wu. By the end of the Song dynasty, there was a scholar named Càn who, during a time of war, moved to Tán Lake in Chángzhōu and became a resident of Chángzhōu again. Mr. Lu's family originally lived in Tán Lake, and he often studied in Zhen Mountain. Scholars respected him and called him "Mr. Zhen Mountain." During the peak of the Lu family, their influence was nearly overwhelming the entire county. His uncle, who held the position of Vice Tutor and had established great achievements, was once a high-ranking official, serving as Chief of the Ministry of Works and Grand Marshal, and was highly esteemed throughout the land. Mr. Lu, as a student, never attached himself to his uncle. When his uncle fell, Mr. Lu alone remained pure and upright, writing scholarly works based on Confucian classics and literature, which were praised by Vice Tutor Wang Wénkègōng, who said, "This young man will surpass me." He then presented his writings to Mr. Lu for review, noting any differences in their interpretations. Wang Wénkègōng said, "Mr. Lu, you should say this." After Wang Wénkègōng's death, Mr. Lu had not yet left his status as a student. He did not name himself after this, but he passed the provincial examination in Yīngtiān and the metropolitan examination twice, excelling in the Spring and Autumn Annals among the candidates. He compared his writings with those of former Chancellor Shí Wénjiègōng Bǎo.

    218

    大竒之拔寘第一而公乡中有忌者匿公策他所俟且半而后出之石公大恨曰吾为人所卖公闻之亦不肯名石公客而以选改翰林庶吉士凡七试皆居第一经生慕称之谓王文恪公复出而其谈说古文辞者以公伯仲庐陵眉山公意殊不自怿惟究心经世之学学成当授官首辅杨文襄公试之复拟第一留充史馆议礼诸臣骤贵而张桂尤幸用事至各为密疏以诸吉士皆前相宏私人也于是上惑之俾各出就选人格而公尚以髙第补工科给事中公拜给事中之三日即上疏言京边诸要务因及人才学术国家理乱大筴杨公读而叹曰吾向者以文士待陆生几失之贾长沙之俦生家敬舆未论

    Explanation

    218

    Dai Qi was placed first in a certain ranking, and in Gong's hometown, there were those who were jealous of him. They hid Gong's strategy somewhere else and waited until almost halfway through the process before revealing it. Shi Gong was greatly resentful, saying, "I was betrayed by someone." Gong heard about it and would not call Shi Gong a guest. Instead, he changed to be selected as a Hanlin Scholar. In all seven examinations, he ranked first. Scholars admired and praised him, calling him Wang Wenke Gong. Those who discuss and write ancient literary works compared him to Ouyang Xiu and Su Shi, but Gong felt quite dissatisfied. He focused solely on practical learning, and after completing his studies, he was to be appointed to an official position. Premier Yang Tinghe tested him again and proposed him as first, keeping him to serve in the History Office as an advisor on rituals. He became suddenly wealthy and powerful, with Zhang Gui being particularly favored and wielding influence. Each wrote secret memorials, stating that all the scholars were former associates of Premier Hong. The emperor was deceived by this and ordered them to step down from their positions. But Gong was still appointed to a high-ranking position as a Censor of the Board of Works. On the third day after Gong took office as Censor, he immediately submitted a memorial discussing important matters concerning the capital and border regions, and also touched upon talent, learning, and the great strategies for governing the state and maintaining order. Yang Gong read it and sighed, saying, "I treated Lu Gong as just a literary scholar, nearly missing out on his true potential. He is like Jia Yi, but his family's respect for him is not discussed."

    219

    也公益感奋复上时政四事皆时所不易言者月中疏凡三上杨公愈欲一当公公愈自引避竟不肯名杨公客寻奉勅清坊廐马公验其非壮者疏请斥市之岁可省刍粟直数万中贵人不便之为蜚语中公公发其奸因条列牧政十事皆报可诸阉气夺初用京朝官主各省试公首得浙江所收多名士其程义雅驯为天下冠还守故职时庆阳伯之奴张与他人斗而其人不胜輙杀其母将以诬张法司谳得之且丽大辟矣其人廹则行赂东厂而庆阳伯康陵之外家也大阉窥上心内薄之即疏证张实殴其母死非其子杀欲以动上果下三法司会谳而都御史熊恭肃公等力持前狱上怒为夺熊公官余

    Explanation

    219

    Increasingly moved and inspired, the official submitted three memorials in total, each addressing four important political issues that were difficult to express. Yang Gong, a high-ranking official, wanted to take charge of the matter, but the official withdrew himself voluntarily, refusing to name Yang Gong's guest. Soon after, the official was ordered to inspect the stables of the imperial palace. Upon investigation, he found that the horses were not fit for service and submitted a memorial requesting their dismissal, which could save tens of thousands of units of fodder and grain annually. The eunuchs found this inconvenient and spread slander against the official. The official exposed their deceit and listed ten administrative policies, all of which were approved. The eunuchs' morale was broken. Initially, officials from the capital were appointed to oversee the provincial examinations. The official was the first to oversee the examination in Zhejiang, where he collected many distinguished scholars. The exam papers were elegant and refined, considered the best in the world. He returned to his former position. At that time, a servant of the noble titled "Prince of Qingyang" named Zhang fought with someone else, and the other party, unable to withstand the fight, killed his mother, intending to frame Zhang. The judicial authorities investigated and found this to be true, sentencing the person to death. The other party, pressured, bribed the Eastern Depot, and the noble's family was involved. The eunuchs, spying on the emperor's mind and seeing his disapproval, submitted evidence showing that Zhang actually beat his mother to death, not his son, and tried to influence the emperor. Indeed, the case was sent to the three judicial authorities for joint investigation, and the high-ranking official Xiong Gong, known for his integrity, strongly maintained the previous verdict. The emperor, angered, revoked Xiong Gong's position, and there were other consequences.

    220

    镌秩有差公抗章谓熊公不当夺官狱不当反厂阉不当狥其辞甚危上怒逮公廷杖之创愈奉职如故张桂时并相挟上眷事欲凌杨公杨公扼腕而已而桂尤鸷寻与张角宠而孽公奋曰决痈者乗其将溃此非时耶夜草疏千余言力陈二相之横以为不蚤去必乱天下其攻桂尤切方属草而鬼啸于庭公叱曰非二氏家鬼耶何自阻我草具亟上之上大感动为罢张桂而特镌桂保傅以示倾听公然犹难辅臣体置公狱将薄儆之而会詹事霍文敏公韬者故与张桂议礼合而以疏辞位为上所重既失党而孤且疑出杨公旨遂抗章湼杨公而为张桂鸣不平上乃召张公俾入辅还桂所镌保傅狱成公

    Explanation

    220

    The official submitted a memorial, arguing that Xiong Gong should not be stripped of his office, the case should not be reversed, and Zhang Gui should not follow his arguments, which were very dangerous. The emperor was greatly angered and ordered the official to be beaten in public. After recovering from the injuries, the official resumed his duties as before. Zhang Gui, at the time serving alongside another prime minister and enjoying the emperor's favor, sought to dominate Yang Gong. Yang Gong was indignant but could do nothing. Zhang Gui was especially fierce and soon gained favor with Zhang Jiao, who was also involved in intrigues. The official then declared, "To resolve a problem at the moment it is about to burst forth—this is the right time!" At night, he drafted a memorial of over a thousand words, strongly criticizing the two prime ministers for their arrogance and arguing that if not removed early, they would surely cause chaos in the world. His attack on Zhang Gui was particularly sharp. Just as he was finishing the draft, ghosts howled in the courtyard. The official rebuked them, saying, "Are you not the spirits of Buddhism and Taoism? Why are you obstructing me?" He quickly submitted the memorial. The emperor was greatly moved and dismissed Zhang Gui, while specially demoting Zhang Gui's position as a tutor or protector to show that he was listening. The official was still difficult to handle as a prime minister and was put in prison, but the emperor intended to punish him lightly as a warning. Meanwhile, the Grand Secretary Ho Wenmin, who was also known as Taowei, had previously agreed with Zhang Gui on ritual matters and had submitted a memorial requesting to step down from office, which the emperor valued. Now, having lost support and become isolated, he suspected that the accusation against Yang Gong came from Yang Gong's instructions. He submitted a memorial accusing Yang Gong and speaking out against Zhang Gui's injustice. The emperor then summoned Zhang Gong and made him an advisor, while returning Zhang Gui's position as a tutor or protector. The case against the official was concluded.

    221

    得重杖谪贵州都匀驿丞而杨公蒙不韪以行事体变矣当公之疏行辇下大小无不加额颂圣明而目公竒男子至是则俱奄然索矣有泣于都门外者都匀在深山中公裹创以单车至驿不携家惟读书而已所创述一出人争传之以拟故王文成公为迁客冠冕居歳余量移江西永新令永新方若刼而乐讼以讼为生公至首严保甲法什五相诇察奸无所容公钩得舍刼者主名凡数家出不意扑禽之而其最雄者彭震徙党数百千出没邻郡不可究捕公念郡狱有大侠故尝与震好能知其处召谓曰若欲生乎欲生为我縳震来因授以方畧使壮士数辈挟之往遂縳震又计縳舍刼之豪左氏者皆毙

    Explanation

    221

    He received a severe flogging and was demoted to be the postmaster of Duyun, Guizhou. Yang Gong Menglai did not agree with the conduct, and the situation changed. When the official's petition was presented at the imperial court, both high and low praised the emperor's wisdom and regarded him as a remarkable man. But by then, everyone had suddenly lost interest. There were those who wept at the city gate. Duyun is deep in the mountains; the official wrapped his wounds and arrived at the post station alone by a single carriage, without taking his family, and only read books. His works were immediately sought after and compared to the works of Wang Wenqing, the former official, and he was regarded as the paragon of exiled officials. After staying there for over a year, he was transferred to be the magistrate of Yongxin, Jiangxi. In Yongxin, the people were prone to litigation and made a living through lawsuits. When the official arrived, he first strictly enforced the household registration system, with every ten households mutually investigating each other, leaving no room for evil. He uncovered the names of the leaders of several bandit groups and, unexpectedly, captured them. The most powerful among them, Peng Zhen, had a gang of hundreds or even thousands who roamed neighboring prefectures and could not be thoroughly captured. The official thought that there was a great hero in the prefectural prison who had once been friends with Peng Zhen and knew his whereabouts. He summoned the man and said, "Do you want to live? If you want to live, help me capture Peng Zhen. I will then give you instructions on how to do it." He sent several strong men with him, and they succeeded in capturing Peng Zhen. He then devised a plan to capture the leader of another bandit group, the Left Clan, and they were all killed.

    222

    于狱讼师积猾横行里中有名为彪为虎者公悉捕执之大者死小者戍其它断孤产辨寃狱一邑惊服以为神明公乃曰害去矣吾可进之教纂曰礼仪式杂他条约数十讲之学宫而后布之士民欢趋若流水他邑视以为师帅台荐娄腾公念太夫人老而相张方在事曰我犹堕彼睚眦宁独寛我上疏拂衣归台使者如失左右手吏民以数万计哭而挽之莫得也相与貌公像其纪思称颂之文至成巻帙公归杜门无事多购异书习读之于刘向所谓七畧者其校雠几徧以至老释方伎黄衣稗官之书无不通晓尤精先朝掌故家言与客谈纚纚若贯珠其披摘精义指踪经畧尤出人意表客亡不怳然自

    Explanation

    222

    During the reign of a certain official, corrupt lawyers and cunning individuals were rampant in the community, causing chaos. There were those known as "tigers" and "panthers" who wreaked havoc. The official captured and detained all of them, executing the more serious offenders and sending the less serious ones to serve in the military. He also resolved property disputes involving orphans and clarified wrongful cases, earning the whole county's admiration and reverence, as if he were a deity. The official then said, "The harm is gone. I can now advance with teaching." He compiled dozens of miscellaneous rituals, ceremonies, and regulations, taught them in the academy, and then disseminated them to scholars and common people. The scholars and common people eagerly followed his teachings like a stream, and other counties regarded him as a teacher. The provincial governor repeatedly recommended him, but the official considered his elderly mother and the chancellor Zhang Fang being in charge, saying, "I am still subject to his petty grudges. How could he alone be lenient with me?" He submitted a memorial and retired. The provincial governor felt as if he had lost his right hand, and officials and commoners, numbering in the tens of thousands, cried and tried to stop him, but they could not. They collectively portrayed him in images, and the writings praising and commemorating him became voluminous. The official retired, closed the door, and had nothing to do, buying many rare books and studying them. He nearly completed the collation and verification of Liu Xiang's "Seven Catalogues," and understood all Daoist, Buddhist, medical, and miscellaneous texts. He was especially proficient in the anecdotes and traditions of the previous dynasty, speaking fluently and coherently with guests. He extracted profound meanings and pointed out strategies and tactics, often beyond people's expectations. Visitors were all deeply impressed and enlightened.

    失也公于文本出左氏太史公然不求肖似辞达为诣人始目公以庐陵眉山为不能尽公然要之有互发者其节奏经纬斐然成章则犹西京之辙也诗虽匠心而冷然见古调不欲与俗酬倡是以篇仆差少岂曰邾郐无讥已耶盖尝脔知鼎矣台察监司郡邑守令时时造公请质公一切不为私居间至于闾左利弊国计大小未尝有所避隠也语及忠孝节义娓娓流羡至切齿污浊眉宇壮色犹隠隠于是听者服公之公而忘其激慰荐之剡积可十百而公絶不通燕中贵人问而当其论糺张桂时中尝訾及分宜氏更分宜贵用事重于二人其修隙又倍之公以故卒不用霍公者颇自悔恨前事尝荐十余

    Explanation

    223

    The text loses its fairness because it is based on the Zuo Zhuan (Commentary of Zuo) and the historian's work, openly not seeking similarity in words to convey the meaning, which is the essence of the text. It is said that Mr. Gong from Luling and Meishan cannot fully express this, but it is openly required to have mutual development, with a rhythm and structure that stands out, like the tracks of the Western Capital. Although the poetry is crafted with care, it coldly reveals the ancient style. It does not wish to engage in the vulgar exchange, hence the number of poems is relatively few. How can it be said that there is no criticism of Zou and Guo? It is said that he has tasted the broth of the cauldron. Officials from the imperial inspection, provincial governors, and local magistrates often visit him to seek his advice, and he does not act out of personal interest. Regarding the benefits and drawbacks of the common people and the national affairs, he never hides anything. When speaking of loyalty, filial piety, integrity, and righteousness, he speaks eloquently and passionately, to the point where he becomes indignant at corruption, his face showing a stern expression. Hence, those who listen admire his fairness and forget his enthusiasm. Recommendations have accumulated in the tens and hundreds, but he refuses to communicate with powerful figures in Yan. When he discusses matters, he criticizes Zhang Gui and others, even mentioning the powerful family of Fen Yi. His grievances are even greater, and he ultimately does not use Huo Gong, who regrets his past actions and has recommended more than ten people.

    贤者而公与焉又使人郑重通殷勤公不许曰天下事佹为若败而何污我也霍公亦不以望公人两贤之公事太夫人孝甚老而失明所以娱悦之者万方晚节稍稍具声伎以共膳饮人或误訾公及太夫人捐馆舍即日开阁散遣之时公且耳顺矣哀毁踰节遂委顿以暨不起病既革顾影叹曰虚有此七尺不以马革裹而朽之牖下也呜呼盖可悲已公为人疎眉目美须骨棱棱起读书视微短然数行俱下于伯氏字而敬其供养孀姊亚于母又为表其节行而传之季采材而不获庸以死公痛之形神为减损与人交有终始善故徐训导元禄则力拔之诸生中岁时恤其家又善故史丞■〈斌上金下〉

    Explanation

    224

    A virtuous person was involved with the public, and someone earnestly conveyed goodwill, but the public did not permit it, saying, "If one acts rashly, what if it fails? It would defile me." Huo Gong also did not expect this of the public. Two virtuous individuals were respected. The public was very filial to his mother or elder, who became blind in old age. To entertain her, he did so in many ways. In his later years, he gradually provided musical entertainment to share meals. Someone mistakenly criticized the public and his mother or elder, and upon their passing, he immediately dismissed his servants or attendants. At the time, the public was approaching the age of sixty, when one can hear things without being offended. His grief and sorrow exceeded the norm, and he became so weak that he could not rise. When his illness reached its final stage, he looked at his reflection and sighed, "To have this seven-foot body in vain, not to die on the battlefield and decay under the window!" Alas, it is indeed sad. The public had sparse eyebrows and eyes, a beautiful beard, and prominent bones. He read carefully but briefly, able to read several lines at a time. He respected and supported his widowed sister, who was second only to his mother. He commended her virtues and passed them on. His younger brother, who sought materials, did not succeed and died. The public mourned deeply, and his body and spirit were diminished. He had consistent and lasting relationships with people. He kindly and patiently instructed someone named Yuanlu, striving to surpass his fellow students. During festivals, he cared for his family. He kindly treated someone with the title of "史丞" and the name "■〈斌上金下〉."

    缓急未尝以匮辞竟以女归其子然二君皆乡长者陆长史锺张郎中约老而慕公义请以身后托公许之没□□□□存恤其子孙毕身无怠公为诸生及仕宦□□□□□王文恪杨文襄二公客然时耿耿不忘□□□□□知已而谓故鸿胪丞钱公贵其童子师也图像□□□时致酒脯焉华学士子潜公同馆友也慕公敬间有所资给公称心而取亦不谢其令永新而尚书尹公台者方籍公交车公异之与深语遂为莫逆晚年世贞以童子见公饮之酒曰是非凡儿也迨叨第进士以书上公公降辞报纳良至盖公卒之十年而其子延枝始以彭征君状乞尹公志其葬又六年而庄皇帝即位诏録故

    225

    In times of urgency, the person never declined due to lack of resources, and ultimately fulfilled a promise by giving his daughter to his son. Both individuals were respected elders in their community, and as they grew older, they admired the public justice of the person. They asked to entrust their affairs to the person after their death, and he agreed. After their death, he took care of their descendants throughout his life without slackening. The person was a student and later an official, and was a guest or associate of the prominent officials Weng Wenke and Yang Wenxiang. Yet, he always remembered them with a sense of urgency or concern. As friends, they considered the former Honglu Chancellor, Qian Gong, to be the teacher of their children. They made a portrait and offered wine and meat as a tribute. Hua Xueshi, Ziqian Gong, was a fellow scholar in the same academy, and they admired and respected the person, sometimes providing financial support. The person took what they needed without hesitation and did not thank them. He was appointed to a new position in Yongxin, and the Shangshu, Yin Gongtai, was involved in the process. The person found him remarkable, engaged in deep conversation, and became close friends. In his later years, Shi Zhen saw the person as a child, gave him wine, and said, "This is no ordinary child." Upon becoming a Jinshi, he wrote a letter to the person. The person humbly replied, accepting the letter with great respect. Ten years after the person's death, his son, Yanyan, began to write a memorial for the deceased, seeking Yin Gong's help to record his burial. Six years later, when the Emperor Zhuang ascended the throne, he issued a decree to record the person's deeds.

    言事者公以尝迁他官格小迕又二载而华公明之台台具以请天子特赠公太常少卿所以褒嘉甚至又二十年而延枝始以墓碑请曰吾父实识君微君文后世谁知有陆太常者余谢曰微余文后世谁不知有陆给事者虽然于谊不可辞公以弘治甲寅生卒以嘉靖辛亥春秋五十有八父曰如隠居士应宾毋即胡太夫人娶盛氏故右都御史应期女子一即延枝娶毛氏女二长适太常少卿王有壬次适太学生史访即■〈斌上金下〉子也孙男二女二所著有春秋镌附注胡传释诗文奏议烟霞山房书尺庚已编若干巻未成者见闻随笔钩玄抉秘礼史二记注释又若干巻墓在贞山之南黄村

    The person who spoke on matters of state, after having been transferred to another position, was slightly at odds. After another two years, Mr. Huaming of the platform formally requested, and the emperor specially bestowed upon him the title of Taichang Shaoying, thereby honoring and commending him even more. Another twenty years later, Yanzhi finally requested a tombstone, saying, "My father truly knew you. Without your writings, future generations would not know of the existence of Mr. Lu." I replied, "Without my writings, who would not know of the existence of Mr. Lu?" Although in terms of propriety, I cannot decline. You were born in the year Jia Yin of the Hongzhi era and died in the year Xin Hai of the Jiajing era, at the age of fifty-eight. Your father, Mr. Ru, the recluse, responded to the guest, "Not to be." You married into the Sheng family, and your wife was the daughter of the former Right Censor-in-Chief Yingqi. You had one daughter, named Yanzhi, who married into the Mao family. Your tomb is located in the village of Huang in the south of Zhen Mountain.

    辞曰三吴靡靡善脂突公于其间独挺发贬剥百子祢经术丘迁风神固为骨下视修轼时入出褒然公交车思郁浡为郎荷櫜备琐闼口嘘长虹贯日月柄臣逡廵魄几夺浮云为纩帝聪窒臣不肤立宁寸裂鬼方遥遥天一髪信心而行瘴为豁永新大豪若距脱爱养单赤佹得活飘然奉身返岩穴焉能为官中屈节飞黄骨腾未秣刷甫驰康庄遂一蹶长离横空翮中铩即羽可仪亦空设煌煌金书下丹阙太常穹碑际云揭呜呼陆公真人杰髙岸可谷名不灭

    Explanation

    227

    The poem says: In the luxurious and decadent lands of the Three Wu, there are those who are smooth-talking and flattering, but among them stands one who boldly criticizes and exposes the flaws of the hundred disciples of Confucian classics, embodying the spirit and style of Confucius and his followers, forming the backbone of the scholarly tradition. From a high position, he looks down upon the imperial carriage, his thoughts deeply troubled. As a low-ranking official, he carries a bag, yet his words are as powerful as a long rainbow piercing the sun and moon. The ministers hesitate, and his spirit is almost overwhelmed, treating clouds as cotton, the emperor's wisdom obstructed, unable to stand firm, not even a small tear can be spared. The distant and mysterious places are far away, and the smallest unit of measurement is insignificant. He acts with faith and confidence, clearing away the mist and fog, the powerful figures in Yongxin resist and reject, caring for the poor and simple, barely managing to survive, retreating to a secluded place, how could one bend their principles to serve in an official position. The horse named Feihuang, symbolizing rapid success or promotion, rides without feeding the horse, falling from a smooth and prosperous path, a bird's wing being broken, symbolizing failure or setback. Even if feathers can be used as a model, it is still an empty setup. The imperial decree or edict, written in gold and hung at the palace gates, the large stele or monument in the imperial temple, reaching up to the clouds. Alas, Lu Gong, a true hero, his high moral integrity is unshakable, his name will never fade.

    陆贞山先生粲字子余一字浚明生而朗秀长身玉立美须髯自其诸生时则已为王文恪所赏识曰是子也材非吾翰林所能有也而久之乃举乡试魁其经明年会试复魁其经以进士改翰林庶吉士凡七试皆居首当是时新贵人张桂长翰林先生耻为之寮约诸庶吉士毋得徃见张桂衔之中于上谓皆故相费公宏桃李以故当散馆公仍试第一而仅得工科给事中先生上言请开弘文馆与博闻有道之士讲说政术开太学举贡诸生
    途与进士并王国及教官材者一体迁擢士毋得乞逺方逺方二千石以上毋以不及调行太仆苑马盐运毋以下考迁又上久任使慎考察汰冗官复制科四事皆次第采纳故相杨公一清见辄叹曰子自爱真经世才也先生以是益自信论事亡所避主浙江试还而法司与厂卫狱互异上右厂卫至为斥台长浃先生力信其不可以是得上怒下诏狱杖三十寻释之时张与桂俱继相拟杨公后先生遂露章劾其奸上为之罢二相一时朝廷

    Explanation

    228

    Mr. Cai of Lu Zhen Shan, courtesy names Zi Yu and Jun Ming, was born with a clear and distinguished appearance, tall and graceful like jade, with a beautiful beard. From his days as a student, he was already admired by Wang Wenke, who said, "This man is so talented that even our Hanlin Academy cannot contain him." After a long time, he won the top position in the provincial examination and the next year in the metropolitan examination, both times topping the exams with his Jinshi degree. He was then appointed as a junior scholar in the Hanlin Academy, passing all seven examinations with top honors. At that time, a newly ennobled Zhang Gui, who had risen in the Hanlin Academy, felt ashamed to be his subordinate and ordered the junior scholars not to visit him. Zhang Gui reported this to the emperor, saying that all were former students of the late prime minister Feng Gonghong. When it came time to disband the scholars, Cai still passed the exam first but was only given the position of Censor of the Engineering Department. Mr. Cai then submitted a memorial to the emperor, proposing the establishment of the Hall of Broad Learning to discuss political strategies with scholars of extensive knowledge and virtue, the reopening of the Imperial Academy to promote scholars, Jinshi degree holders, and capable teachers and officials together, and the prohibition of scholars requesting positions in remote areas. Officials in remote areas with a rank of 2,000 stones or higher should not be transferred due to insufficiency, and officials in charge of the imperial stables, horse breeding, and salt transportation should not be promoted based on poor performance. He also proposed reinstating the four types of special examinations. All these suggestions were gradually adopted. Yang Gong Yiqing, upon seeing him, would often say, "Take care of yourself; truly a talent for governing the world." Mr. Cai became even more confident and spoke freely and openly. After presiding over the Zhejiang examination and returning, the judicial department and the eunuch-controlled secret police had conflicting reports. The emperor favored the eunuch-controlled secret police and rebuked the chief censor. Mr. Cai strongly believed in the correctness of his position, which angered the emperor, who ordered him to be beaten thirty times and then released. At that time, Zhang and Gui both succeeded to high positions, following the example of Yang Gong. Mr. Cai then publicly submitted a memorial to impeach their corruption, and the emperor dismissed the two chancellors, causing a significant stir in the court.

    肃然望治而上寻入霍詹事韬语谓先生缘杨公指于是首召张而杨公不自安更请去而先生再下诏狱以谪矣先生之自都镇驿丞迁永新令有善政其士民多讴謡之而念相张数踬起不能不相中且母老上书乞致仕归里居凡十八年中外论荐者无虑三十疏而皆报罢先生以母老多戚戚为选声色以娱之而已不能无染指及母死悉屏去之先生性伉爽每语意气及不平事慷慨攘臂须尽张其为文精雅有法得班氏及韩欧遗意
    赞曰其貌余识之朗乎其欲仙其辨余听之沛乎其河悬其文余读之后永叔而前孟坚其志余窥之宁璧碎而不瓦完尽其言庶几经世之贾陆老其才将无筹边之范韩也耶（四库全书?集部?别集类?弇州四部稿续稿卷一百四十九）

    陆子余集序（魏学礼）

    Explanation

    229

    Mr. [last name omitted] was invited into the court by Lord Huo Tiao, who spoke of how Mr. [last name omitted] followed the guidance of Yang Gong. He was first summoned to meet Zhang, but Yang Gong became uncomfortable and requested to leave, leading to Mr. [last name omitted] being re-imprisoned and exiled. Mr. [last name omitted] was appointed from the position of postal clerk in the capital to the magistrate of Yongxin, where he implemented good governance. The people of Yongxin sang praises of him, but Zhang repeatedly caused setbacks, leading to mutual resentment. Additionally, due to his mother's old age, he petitioned to retire and returned home, where he lived for eighteen years. During this time, there were no fewer than thirty memorials recommending him, all of which were rejected. Mr. [last name omitted] often felt sad due to his mother's old age and chose entertainment to cheer her up, but he could not avoid being influenced by it. After his mother's death, he completely gave up these entertainments. Mr. [last name omitted] was upright and open-minded, often speaking with enthusiasm and indignation, and writing with precision and elegance, following the styles of Ban Gu and Han Yu and Ouyang Xiu. His appearance was clear and ethereal, his eloquence powerful, his writing refined, and his aspirations noble. His words and talents are comparable to those of famous historical figures.

    Commentary: Mr. [last name omitted]'s appearance was clear and ethereal, his eloquence powerful, his writing refined, and his aspirations noble. His words and talents are comparable to those of famous historical figures. (The Four Treasuries, Collection Department, Miscellaneous Collection Class, Yan Zhou Four Departments Continued Draft Volume 149)

    Mr. [last name omitted]'s Collected Works (Wei Xueli)

    230

    余读陆先生文未尝不太息流涕也曰嗟乎天之重才固若是哉当古昔时葛天大容伊挚公旦召伯仲尼屈平董生桓次公之徒建德植义名称郁流万世师焉紬其末绪固足制理百物靖宁寰海间以圣智昌之文辞蔚乎可览观是数公者固当世贤圣俊豪士也窥其大者固不曰文文则符其德矣然葛天大容伊摰召伯勲铭敦鼎身享富厚显荣而毋疵厉譬若神龙升于青霄之上云蒸雾合衡变化而从巻舒遭会遇际顾不休与及至公旦仲尼至圣也赤心奉国劳身焦思而横被流言之谤奔走天下执贽谒策者七十二邦而卒无所遇屈平董生桓次公骏雄博辩人也殚精竭思匡正国议批鳞逆心引义固争上前而身横江流废放横沦绌怫以死何其厄与天之生贤圣也葢寡即其人贤圣而流弃不振故曰贤不必用忠不必以悲夫悲夫殆天侈其德故俭其身耶陆先生粲者字子余苏之长洲人也六岁则诵书日数千言诸贤豪长者故从父游时时与语奇爱之欲困以时擿句使对对輙奇敏客惊则谓父曰儿非庸人他日当为

    Explanation

    230

    Whenever I read Mr. Lu's writings, I never fail to sigh and shed tears, exclaiming, "Alas, how heavily does Heaven burden talent!" In ancient times, figures such as Getaitian, Dairong, Yizhi, Gongdan, Zhaobo, Zhongni (Confucius), Qu Yuan, Dongseng (Dong Zhongshu), and Huan Cizi established virtue and upheld righteousness, their names and reputations flourishing as eternal teachers. Tracing their legacy, they were sufficient to govern all things and bring peace to the world, interwoven with the wisdom and brilliance of their writings, which are rich and worthy of contemplation. These individuals were indeed the wise, saintly, and noble heroes of their time. When one looks at their grand achievements, it is not merely their literary accomplishments that are remarkable; their writings reflect their virtues. Yet, Getaitian, Dairong, Yizhi, and Zhaobo, despite their illustrious deeds inscribed in bronze vessels and their wealth and honor, were free from blemish and adversity, like a divine dragon ascending to the heavens, where clouds and mists gather, and transformations occur, yet they move gracefully and freely, encountering good fortune without end. But when it came to Gongdan and Zhongni, the most sage of all, they served their country with a sincere heart, toiling and pondering tirelessly, yet they were slandered by malicious rumors, traveling to seventy-two states, offering their credentials and strategies, yet ultimately finding no success. Qu Yuan, Dongseng, and Huan Cizi, though they were skilled and eloquent individuals, exhausted their minds and thoughts to correct state affairs, challenging the powerful and speaking out for righteousness, yet they were exiled, humiliated, and died in despair. How tragic! Heaven seems to bestow great virtue but spares the individual. Perhaps Heaven bestows great virtue but spares the individual.

    Mr. Lu Càn, styled Ziyu, was from Changzhou in Suzhou. At the age of six, he could recite thousands of words daily. He was often taken by his father to visit various scholars and dignitaries, engaging in conversations with them. They found him extraordinary and intelligent, often challenging him with difficult phrases to test his wit. Whenever he responded, he did so with remarkable sensitivity and quickness, surprising guests, who would then tell his father, "This child is no ordinary person; he will surely achieve great things in the future."

    231

    天下士先生问师汉髙祖何如人哉师曰寛仁大度之主也先生曰然则何以杀韩彭师惊怪其明悟而谓先生父曰吾不能为若师也遂谢罢去九岁能着文十四岁选入邑校嘉靖乙酉乡举丙戌举于大宗伯皆奏名冠经生天子临轩策试上第选翰林吉士阁试第一者八昔所未有当是时贵宠用事者操势轩轾人朝中皆震恐先生嫉其阿谀希世不肯见见弗之揖以故用事者不恱则以为工科给事数上书言事诸所建白语在奏议中顷之有寃狱中贵人持之坚魏郎中应召执法争之御史大夫熊浃是魏君议上怒罢浃谪魏君戍公卿皆不敢言先生上章谏天子震怒搒笞之输以鬼薪寻赦之复疏辅臣张璁桂蕚罪上方倚用两人两人者尊倨甚公卿皆侧目而视疏奏羣臣皆惊惧且不测赖天子明圣为罢璁蕚而已上曰璁蕚有罪粲疏何暮也左迁都匀驿丞寻迁永新令邑中称为神明居亡何乞归养母天子报可竟以母养致惫而死夫竭身以事父母为臣不避险难可谓臣子矣先生之细行不采即其言论风指足以概

    Explanation

    231

    A Scholar from All Under Heaven asked the teacher about Emperor Han Gaozu, "What kind of person was he?" The teacher replied, "He was a ruler of broad-minded benevolence." The scholar then asked, "Then why did he kill Han Xin and Peng Yue?" The teacher was surprised by the scholar's insight and told his father, "I cannot be his teacher." He then dismissed the scholar. At the age of nine, the scholar could write essays. At fourteen, he was selected to enter the local school. During the Jiajing era, he passed the provincial examination in 1535 and the metropolitan examination in 1536, both ranking first. The emperor personally presided over the imperial examination, and the scholar ranked first, becoming a Hanlin Academy scholar. There were eight first-place winners in the Hanlin Academy examination, an unprecedented achievement at that time. At that time, powerful and influential figures wielded their power to sway others, causing fear among officials. The scholar despised their flattery and refused to bow to them. As a result, the powerful figures did not like him and appointed him as a censor in the Department of Works. He frequently submitted memorials and proposed various matters, all of which are recorded in his official documents. Soon after, there was a miscarriage of justice where powerful figures stubbornly defended the guilty. Wei, the magistrate of the Ministry of Justice, was summoned to enforce the law and argued against it. The Chief Censor Xiong Jia supported Wei's argument, and the emperor became angry, dismissing Xiong Jia and exiling Wei to the frontier. The high officials were all too afraid to speak out. The scholar then submitted a memorial to advise the emperor, who became furious and punished him with corporal punishment, forcing him to perform labor for the imperial household. He was later pardoned. He then submitted another memorial against the powerful ministers Zhang Cong and Gui E, who were heavily relied upon by the emperor. The two ministers were very arrogant, and the high officials all looked at them with fear and respect. When the scholar's memorial was presented, all the ministers were shocked and feared the worst. Fortunately, the emperor was wise and just, and dismissed Zhang Cong and Gui E. The emperor said, "Zhang Cong and Gui E have committed crimes, but why did you submit the memorial so late?" The scholar was demoted to the position of postal station official in Duyun, and later promoted to the position of magistrate of Yongxin County, where he was called a "god" by the people. Soon after, he requested to return home to care for his mother. The emperor granted his request, and he eventually died from exhaustion while caring for his mother. To serve one's parents with all one's strength, a subject does not avoid danger and hardship, which can truly be called the duty of a subject. Although the scholar's minor actions were not recorded, his words and writings are sufficient to summarize his character.

    232

    其为人文辞行之华也华而少实君子病焉先生则华实兼至者也今览观其文章载事之辞则师司马子长而直鲠毋所诎时时兼用韩生诸子而与吕侍御书则效宋齐人铭辞毎古简而福济观辞则闳丽肆逸大类山玄卿五言古诗效选律絶则多效白甫其声亷其节哀其词宏绰而庄郁信乎善文哉儒生白首章句语之古辞则鄂眙而相顾先生顾独尊好古书数规摹之非洪儒巨彦聪智轶奇者其能是哉余少而好慕奇节毎读书至忠臣孝子未尝不废书泣涕也魏郎中者礼季父也以故余知先生之事独详而心窃敬之顾少且贱不获执鞭先生之门先生没而其子某以先生之文视余请序之余因慨然叹曰天之生才若先生者葢寡而绩功弗竟抑郁以死是天重才而轻禄爵也非耶当先生论狱得谴裁获少假复靡大吏之吻身乃蹷焉非壮烈节士哉文奚足以尽先生亦足明其志藻矣葢仲尼之训曰有德者必有言千载而后考先生之德者于言亦见其微哉（四库全书?集部?总集类?明文海卷二百四十八）

    Explanation

    232

    His literary works are splendid in form but lack substance, which troubles the virtuous. The master, however, combines both form and substance. Now, upon examining his writings and the words he uses to describe events, one can see that he follows the style of Sima Qian, being straightforward and unyielding, and occasionally incorporates the styles of Han Yu and other scholars. In his letters to Lord Lu, he imitates the inscriptions of the Song and Qi dynasties, which are often concise and ancient. His writings on blessings and condolences are grand, beautiful, unrestrained, and free-flowing, much like the five-character ancient poems of Shan Xuanying. When imitating the regulated verse and absolute couplets, he often follows Bai Juyi. His voice is clear, his tone is mournful, and his words are grand, expansive, and solemn. Indeed, he is a masterful writer. Scholars who have reached old age and are familiar with ancient texts will be amazed and look at each other. The master alone reveres and studies ancient books, setting an example that only great scholars with extraordinary wisdom can achieve. I, when young, admired unusual virtues. Whenever I read about loyal ministers and filial sons, I could not help but put down the book and weep. Lord Wei, who was my father's younger brother, therefore I knew the master's affairs in great detail and secretly admired him. However, being young and of low status, I did not have the opportunity to serve at the master's door. After the master's death, his son presented his writings to me and asked for a preface. I then sighed and said, "Heaven rarely produces talents like the master, and his achievements were not completed; he died in depression. Is this not Heaven valuing talent over rank and position?" When the master was accused and punished, he received little mercy from the officials, and his body was broken. Was he not a valiant and righteous man? Can writing fully express the master? It is sufficient to clarify his aspirations and brilliance. Confucius said, "Those with virtue will have words." A thousand years later, when examining the master's virtue through his words, one can see its subtlety.

    233

    陆氏【粲】左传附注
    五卷
    存
    春秋左氏镌
    二卷
    未见
    粲自序曰太史迁言仲尼成春秋鲁君子左邱明受之为着传余以为非也左氏之文闳丽巨衍为百代取则然其指意所存乃往往卑贱不中于道或为竒言怪说颇骛乎末流矣盖战国之初有私淑于七十子之徒者不得与仲尼并时又其书遭秦伏隐及汉世晚立于学官自刘歆始定其章句吾疑歆辈以意附益之者多也作左氏春秋镌以晓初学者令观择焉

    Explanation

    233

    Lu Cian, in his Left Transmission with Annotations, wrote:
    Five volumes
    Exist
    Carved Spring and Autumn Left Transmission
    Two volumes
    Not seen
    In his own preface, he said: "Master Sima Qian said that Confucius completed the Spring and Autumn Annals, and Lu's gentleman, Zuo Qiuming, received it and wrote a commentary. I believe this is not the case. The Zuo Zhuan is magnificent, grand, and extensive, serving as a model for future generations. However, the intentions or meanings contained are often lowly and not in line with the Way, with strange and bizarre statements that stray into the periphery. At the beginning of the Warring States period, there were those who privately admired the teachings of the seventy disciples of Confucius but could not live at the same time as Confucius. His book was hidden during the Qin dynasty and was established in the Han dynasty's academic institutions later. From Liu Xun, who first standardized its chapters and verses, I suspect that many parts were added by Liu Xun and his followers. I wrote Left Transmission Spring and Autumn Carved to enlighten beginners and let them choose what to learn."

    234

    春秋胡传辨疑
    四卷
    未见
    粲自序曰昔仲尼作春秋旨微而显至胡氏说经庶几得之惜其或失之过求辞不厌繁委而圣人之意愈晦矣余尝欲着之论辨而未能也今谪居多暇复披诵其传遇有疑处辄书焉久而成帙以示从游之士多有骇而问者余语之曰吾为此非敢异于胡氏也实不敢异于孔子耳虽然余敢遽以为是哉当质诸深于春秋者傥取二三策乎否则无惑乎诸君病吾言也嘉靖辛夘春二月朔日（四库全书?史部?目录类?经籍之属?经义考卷二百一）

    左传附注
    作者:陆粲（字子余；号不详） （明） 撰　　
    部:经 类:春秋类 属:
    参考数据:（《四库大辞典》）

    Explanation

    234

    Doubts on the Commentary of Hu on the Spring and Autumn Annals
    Four Volumes
    Not Seen
    Lu Can's Preface:
    In the past, Confucius wrote the Spring and Autumn Annals, whose meaning was subtle yet clear. As for Hu Sanxian's commentary on the classics, it nearly grasped the essence, but it sometimes went too far in seeking words and did not tire of being detailed, making the meaning of the sage even more obscure. I once wished to write a discussion on this matter but was unable to do so. Now, during my exile, I have much leisure, and I have again read and studied the commentary. Whenever I encounter doubts, I write them down, and over time, they have accumulated into a collection. I showed this to those who follow and study with me, and many were startled and asked questions. I told them, "I write this not to differ from Hu Sanxian, but I dare not differ from Confucius." However, how dare I immediately consider it correct? I should consult those who are deeply knowledgeable about the Spring and Autumn Annals. Perhaps I will take a few pieces of advice. Otherwise, there is no wonder that my words are criticized.
    Jiajing, year Jia-Mao, the first day of the second month (from the Four Treasuries: History Section, Catalogue of Classics, Volume 201).

    Left Commentary with Annotations
    Author: Lu Can (courtesy name Zi Yu; pseudonym unknown)
    Dynasty: Ming
    Department: Classics
    Category: Spring and Autumn Annals
    Reference: Dictionary of the Four Treasuries

    235

    五卷。陆粲（１4９4—１55１）撰。陆粲字子余，长洲（今江苏省苏州市）人，明代经学家。嘉靖进士，官至工科给事中，挺劲敢言。曾因争张福达狱，下诏狱廷杖。不久又因草疏论张璁、桂萼，谪贵州都镇驿丞，迁永新知县，后以念母辞归。陆氏平生著述，除此书外，还有《春秋胡氏传辨疑》、《左氏春秋镌》。此书前三卷驳正杜预《集解》，第四卷驳正孔颖达《正义》，第五卷驳正陆德明《辞义》。其训诂注意证据，多旁采诸家之论，亦间断以己意。其纠正杜注，有得有失，如成公二年《传》“翦灭此而后朝食”，杜注训翦为尽，陆氏以为翦应训为翦商之翦，义为齐断。并据徐锴《说文系传》引此文作“揃”，乃引《庄子》、《三苍》、《玉篇》、《急就篇》以证二字通假。陆说是。又庄公三十二年《传》“荦有力焉，能投盖于稷门”，杜释盖为覆，云“走而自投，接其屋之桷，反复门上”。陆氏引刘炫说“投车盖过于稷门”以驳正之，陆说较长。

    Explanation

    235

    Five Volumes. Lu Can (1494–1551), author of these works, was a scholar of the Ming Dynasty from Changzhou (now Suzhou, Jiangsu Province). He passed the imperial examinations during the Jiajing era and served as an official in the Ministry of Works, known for his boldness and outspokenness. He was once sent to the Imperial Prison and subjected to public flogging for his involvement in the Zhang Fuda case. Shortly thereafter, he was demoted to the position of magistrate in Duzhen, Guizhou, and later appointed as magistrate in Yongxin, but resigned due to his desire to care for his mother.

    Lu Can's lifetime works, besides this book, include Chunqiu Hu Shizhuan Bianyi (A Critical Analysis of the Chunqiu as Interpreted by Hu Sanxian) and Zuo Shizhuan Juan (Annotations on the Zuo Zhuan). The first three volumes of this work refute Du Yu's Jiji (Collective Commentary) on the Zuo Zhuan, the fourth volume refutes Kong Yingda's Zhengyi (Standard Interpretations) on the Zuo Zhuan, and the fifth volume refutes Lu Deming's Shiyi (Explanations of Words and Sentences) on the Zuo Zhuan. In his textual criticism, Lu Can pays close attention to evidence, often drawing upon the theories of various scholars, and occasionally inserting his own interpretations.

    In correcting Du Yu's annotations, Lu Can has both successes and failures. For example, in the Zuo Zhuan passage from the second year of Duke Cheng's reign, "Jianmie ci erhou chaoshi" (After completely destroying this, one can then eat), Du Yu interprets "jian" as meaning "to exhaust" or "to finish." Lu Can, however, argues that "jian" should be interpreted as "jian shang" (to cut or slash), with the meaning of "to cut or sever equally." He supports this interpretation by citing Xu Kai's Shuowen Xizhuan (Explanations of Characters and Words in the Shuowen Jiezi), which uses the character "jian" in this context. Lu Can then cites Zhuangzi, San Cang (an ancient dictionary), Yupian (an early Chinese dictionary), and Jijuopian (an ancient text for teaching) to demonstrate that the two characters are interchangeable. Lu Can's interpretation is correct.

    In another example, from the Zuo Zhuan passage in the thirty-second year of Duke Zhuang's reign, "Luo youli yan, neng tou gai yu ji men" (Luo was strong and could throw something over the gate of Ji), Du Yu interprets "gai" as meaning "to cover" or "to overlap," describing the act as "running and throwing oneself, so that it covers and overlaps the door lintel." Lu Can, however, cites Liu Xuan's interpretation, "tou chegai guo yu ji men" (throwing the carriage canopy over the gate of Ji), to refute Du Yu's explanation. Lu Can's interpretation is more detailed and persuasive.

    236

    又僖公四年《传》“君处北海，寡人处南海，唯是风马牛不相及也”，杜注云：“马牛风逸，盖未界之微事，故以取喻。”陆引马永卿曰：“注意未明，此乃丑诋之辞尔。言齐楚相远，虽马牛之风者犹不相及，今汝人也，而乃入吾地何也？此风与《书》‘马牛其风’同意，牡牝相诱谓之风。”陆说较长。又宣公十四年《传》“鄙我也。鄙我，亡也”。杜注：“以我比其边鄙，是与亡国同。”陆氏则云：“《吕氏春秋》说此事云：‘往不假道，来不假道，是以宋为野鄙也。

    Explanation

    236

    In the fourth year of Duke Xi's reign, the Zuo Zhuan records: "Your lord resides in the North Sea, and I reside in the South Sea; even if the wind blows, horses and cattle will not come into contact with each other." Du Yu's annotation explains: "Even if horses and cattle are blown by the wind, it is a minor matter that they have not crossed paths, hence the metaphor." Lu Ji, citing Ma Yongqing, states: "The meaning is not clear; this is a derogatory expression. It means that Qi and Chu are so far apart that even if horses and cattle are blown by the wind, they will not meet. Now, you, a person, have entered my territory—why is that? This 'wind' is similar to the phrase '马牛其风' in the Shangshu, which means the attraction between males and females is called 'wind.'" Lu Ji's interpretation is more detailed. In the fourteenth year of Duke Xuan's reign, the Zuo Zhuan records: "I am treated as a borderland. Being treated as a borderland means to be destroyed." Du Yu's annotation explains: "Comparing me to a borderland, it is the same as treating a state as destroyed." Lu Ji, however, says: "The Lüshi Chunqiu explains this saying: 'Neither going nor coming through without permission, thus Song is treated as a borderland.'"

    237

    ’高诱注：‘谓以宋为鄙邑。’今按，鄙当为鄙薄之意”，并引昭公十六年《传》“夫犹鄙我”为证，又引《尚书》伪孔传为证。杜注简易明白，陆之反驳反而横生枝节。总而言之，此书得多失少。清初大学者顾炎武甚推重此书，其《日知录》于驳正《左传注》后附书称，“凡邵陆傅三先生所已辨者不录”。邵指邵宝《左传梼》、傅指傅逊《左传属事》，陆即此书。陆粲又有《左传镌》二卷，认为《左传》为战国时人作，而刘歆又以己意附盖，故往往卑贱不中道，或为奇言怪说，鹜于未流。现存明嘉靖十九年刻本。

    Explanation

    237

    Gao You's annotation states, "To regard Song as a small town." However, it is argued that the character 鄙 should be understood as "humble" or "insignificant," and this interpretation is supported by the reference to the Zuo Zhuan passage "He still considers me humble" and the Shangshu Kongzhuan (Commentary on the Book of Documents by Kong). Du's annotation is simple and clear, while Lu's rebuttal, on the other hand, introduces unnecessary complications. In summary, this book has more merits than flaws. The eminent scholar Gu Yanwu of the early Qing dynasty highly esteemed this book, and in his Rizhilu (Daily Knowledge Record), he noted after criticizing the annotations of the Zuo Zhuan, "All that the three scholars, Shao, Lu, and Fu, have already refuted is not recorded." Shao refers to Shao Bao's Zuo Zhuan Tao (Tao of the Commentary of Zuo), Fu refers to Fu Xun's Zuo Zhuan Shushi (Affairs of the Commentary of Zuo), and Lu refers to this book. Lu Can also wrote Zuo Zhuan Juan (Carving of the Commentary of Zuo) in two volumes, in which he argued that the Zuo Zhuan was written by people of the Warring States period, and that Liu Xin added his own interpretations, resulting in content that is often lowly and not in line with the Way, or filled with strange and bizarre statements, running wild in the end. The surviving edition printed in the nineteenth year of Jiajing in the Ming dynasty is still extant.

    238

    春秋胡氏传辨疑
    作者:陆粲（字子余；号不详） （明） 撰　　
    部:经 类:春秋类 属:
    参考数据:（《四库大辞典》）
    二卷。明陆粲（详见《左传附注》）撰。此书乃驳正《胡传》之作。陆氏认为，胡安国说经，或失之于过求，词不厌烦而孔子之意愈晦。陆氏主张信经而不信例，《自序》云：“昔之君子有言《春秋》无达例，如以例言，则有时而穷。惟有时而穷，故求其说而不得，从而为之辞。”其书体例则先列《胡传》于前，而以己意纠正于后。如胡氏认为《春秋》之所以起于鲁隐公，是因为《春秋》罪周平王，而陆氏则引欧阳修“得鲁史记自隐公至于获麟”之说以驳胡氏。

    Explanation

    238

    Doubts on the Annotations of the Spring and Autumn Annals by Hu
    Author: Lu Can (style name Zi Yu; pseudonym unknown) (Ming Dynasty)
    Category: Classics, Spring and Autumn Annals Class
    Reference: Siku Quanshu Dictionary

    This work consists of two volumes and was written by Lu Can of the Ming Dynasty. It is a critique of Hu's annotations of the Spring and Autumn Annals. Lu Can believed that Hu Anguo's interpretation of the classics sometimes went too far, leading to overly verbose explanations that obscured the meaning of Confucius. Lu Can advocated for believing in the text of the Spring and Autumn Annals rather than in fixed rules. In the preface, he wrote: "Ancient scholars said that the Spring and Autumn Annals have no fixed rules. If one relies on rules, there are times when they are insufficient. Only because there are times when rules are insufficient does one seek explanations and find that they are lacking, thus creating explanations." The structure of the book lists Hu's annotations first and then corrects them with his own interpretations. For example, Hu believed that the Spring and Autumn Annals began with Duke Yin of Lu because it blamed King Ping of Zhou, but Lu Can cited Ouyang Xiu's statement, "Obtained the history of Lu from Duke Yin to the capture of the Qilin," to refute Hu's view.

    239

    又如“隐元年”，胡氏释元为仁，云：“即位之一年必称元年者，明人君之用也。元即仁也。仁，人心也。”陆驳之云，“古者谓首为元，元年者，即位之首年。”又引《周仲称父鼎铭》、《父己鼎铭》、《敔敦铭》以证周之时凡月皆称王，又引《商钟铭》证三代皆称王月，以驳“王正月”出自圣笔的附会之说，极其精当。又如昭公元年“莒展〔舆〕出奔吴”，胡氏云：“展舆曷为以国氏罪诸侯之与其立也。”臆度之甚。陆氏驳之云：“书展舆而不采莒，则无以知其为莒之展舆也，故以国氏。”凡此之类只十余条，大抵皆能切中胡氏之弊。此书作于陆氏谪居贵州都镇驿之时。现存指海本。

    Explanation

    239

    As for "the first year of Duke Yin," Hu Shi interprets "Yuan" as "Ren," saying: "The first year of a reign must be called 'Yuan' to show the use of a ruler. Yuan is Ren. Ren is the human heart." Lu Xiangshan refutes this, stating: "In ancient times, the first was called 'Yuan,' so 'Yuan Year' refers to the first year of a reign." He also cites the inscriptions on the Zhou Zhongchengfu Ding, the Fugiji Ding, and the Yugun to prove that during the Zhou dynasty, all months were called "Wang Month," and he cites the inscription on the Shang Zhong to prove that the Xia, Shang, and Zhou dynasties all used "Wang Month," thus refuting the notion that "Wang Zhengyue" (the first month of the king) is a divine or authoritative term, which is extremely precise. For example, in the first year of Duke Zhao, "Zhan Yu of Qi fled to Wu," Hu Shi says: "Why is Zhan Yu referred to by his state name? Is it to blame the lords for their involvement in his establishment?" This is a great guess. Lu Xiangshan refutes this, saying: "The text mentions Zhan Yu but does not mention Qi, so it is impossible to know that he is Zhan Yu of Qi, hence the use of the state name." All such examples amount to over ten, and in general, they all hit the weaknesses of Hu Shi's interpretation. This book was written when Lu was exiled to Duzhen Yi in Guizhou. The current edition is found in Zhihai.
